To simplify this process, <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">genetic engineering</a> is often used to add chemical features to proteins that make them easier to purify without affecting their structure or activity. Here, a "tag" consisting of a specific amino acid sequence, often a series of <a href="/wiki/Histidine" title="Histidine">histidine</a> residues (a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/His-tag" title="His-tag">His-tag</a>"), is attached to one terminus of the protein. As a result, when the lysate is passed over a chromatography column containing <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a>, the histidine residues ligate the nickel and attach to the column while the untagged components of the lysate pass unimpeded. A number of different tags have been developed to help researchers purify specific proteins from complex mixtures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Terpe2003_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Terpe2003-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cellular_localization">Cellular localization</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Localisations02eng.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="618" data-file-width="469" decoding="async" height="395" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Localisations02eng.jpg/300px-Localisations02eng.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Localisations02eng.jpg/450px-Localisations02eng.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6e/Localisations02eng.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Localisations02eng.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Proteins in different <a href="/wiki/Cellular_compartment" title="Cellular compartment">cellular compartments</a> and structures tagged with <a href="/wiki/Green_fluorescent_protein" title="Green fluorescent protein">green fluorescent protein</a> (here, white)</div></div></div>
<p>The study of proteins <i>in vivo</i> is often concerned with the synthesis and localization of the protein within the cell. Although many intracellular proteins are synthesized in the <a href="/wiki/Cytoplasm" title="Cytoplasm">cytoplasm</a> and membrane-bound or secreted proteins in the <a href="/wiki/Endoplasmic_reticulum" title="Endoplasmic reticulum">endoplasmic reticulum</a>, the specifics of how proteins are <a href="/wiki/Protein_targeting" title="Protein targeting">targeted</a> to specific organelles or cellular structures is often unclear. A useful technique for assessing cellular localization uses genetic engineering to express in a cell a <a href="/wiki/Fusion_protein" title="Fusion protein">fusion protein</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chimera_(protein)" title="Chimera (protein)">chimera</a> consisting of the natural protein of interest linked to a "<a href="/wiki/Reporter_gene" title="Reporter gene">reporter</a>" such as <a href="/wiki/Green_fluorescent_protein" title="Green fluorescent protein">green fluorescent protein</a> (GFP).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stepanenko2008_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stepanenko2008-57">[57]</a></sup> The fused protein's position within the cell can be cleanly and efficiently visualized using <a href="/wiki/Microscopy" title="Microscopy">microscopy</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Yuste2005_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Yuste2005-58">[58]</a></sup> as shown in the figure opposite.
</p><p>Other methods for elucidating the cellular location of proteins requires the use of known compartmental markers for regions such as the ER, the Golgi, lysosomes or vacuoles, mitochondria, chloroplasts, plasma membrane, etc. With the use of fluorescently tagged versions of these markers or of antibodies to known markers, it becomes much simpler to identify the localization of a protein of interest. For example, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indirect_immunofluorescence" title="Indirect immunofluorescence">indirect immunofluorescence</a> will allow for fluorescence colocalization and demonstration of location. Fluorescent dyes are used to label cellular compartments for a similar purpose.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Margolin2000_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Margolin2000-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other possibilities exist, as well. For example, <a href="/wiki/Immunohistochemistry" title="Immunohistochemistry">immunohistochemistry</a> usually utilizes an antibody to one or more proteins of interest that are conjugated to enzymes yielding either luminescent or chromogenic signals that can be compared between samples, allowing for localization information. Another applicable technique is cofractionation in sucrose (or other material) gradients using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isopycnic_centrifugation" title="Isopycnic centrifugation">isopycnic centrifugation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walker2000_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walker2000-60">[60]</a></sup> While this technique does not prove colocalization of a compartment of known density and the protein of interest, it does increase the likelihood, and is more amenable to large-scale studies.
</p><p>Finally, the gold-standard method of cellular localization is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Immunoelectron_microscopy" title="Immunoelectron microscopy">immunoelectron microscopy</a>. This technique also uses an antibody to the protein of interest, along with classical electron microscopy techniques. The sample is prepared for normal electron microscopic examination, and then treated with an antibody to the protein of interest that is conjugated to an extremely electro-dense material, usually gold. This allows for the localization of both ultrastructural details as well as the protein of interest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mayhew2008_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mayhew2008-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>Through another genetic engineering application known as <a href="/wiki/Site-directed_mutagenesis" title="Site-directed mutagenesis">site-directed mutagenesis</a>, researchers can alter the protein sequence and hence its structure, cellular localization, and susceptibility to regulation. This technique even allows the incorporation of unnatural amino acids into proteins, using modified tRNAs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hohsaka2002_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hohsaka2002-62">[62]</a></sup> and may allow the rational <a href="/wiki/Protein_design" title="Protein design">design</a> of new proteins with novel properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cedrone2000_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cedrone2000-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Proteomics">Proteomics</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Proteomics" title="Proteomics">Proteomics</a></div>
<p>The total complement of proteins present at a time in a cell or cell type is known as its <a href="/wiki/Proteome" title="Proteome">proteome</a>, and the study of such large-scale data sets defines the field of <a href="/wiki/Proteomics" title="Proteomics">proteomics</a>, named by analogy to the related field of <a href="/wiki/Genomics" title="Genomics">genomics</a>. Key experimental techniques in proteomics include <a href="/wiki/Two-dimensional_gel_electrophoresis" title="Two-dimensional gel electrophoresis">2D electrophoresis</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gorg2008_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gorg2008-64">[64]</a></sup> which allows the separation of many proteins, <a href="/wiki/Mass_spectrometry" title="Mass spectrometry">mass spectrometry</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Conrotto2008_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Conrotto2008-65">[65]</a></sup> which allows rapid high-throughput identification of proteins and sequencing of peptides (most often after <a href="/wiki/In-gel_digestion" title="In-gel digestion">in-gel digestion</a>), <a href="/wiki/Protein_microarray" title="Protein microarray">protein microarrays</a>, which allow the detection of the relative levels of the various proteins present in a cell, and <a href="/wiki/Two-hybrid_screening" title="Two-hybrid screening">two-hybrid screening</a>, which allows the systematic exploration of <a href="/wiki/Protein%E2%80%93protein_interaction" title="Protein–protein interaction">protein–protein interactions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koegl2007_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Koegl2007-66">[66]</a></sup> The total complement of biologically possible such interactions is known as the <a href="/wiki/Interactome" title="Interactome">interactome</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plewczynski2009_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plewczynski2009-67">[67]</a></sup> A systematic attempt to determine the structures of proteins representing every possible fold is known as <a href="/wiki/Structural_genomics" title="Structural genomics">structural genomics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhang2003_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zhang2003-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure_determination">Structure determination</span></h3>
<p>Discovering the tertiary structure of a protein, or the quaternary structure of its complexes, can provide important clues about how the protein performs its function and how it can be affected, i.e. in <a href="/wiki/Drug_design#Structure-based" title="Drug design">drug design</a>. As proteins are <a href="/wiki/Diffraction-limited_system" title="Diffraction-limited system">too small to be seen</a> under a <a href="/wiki/Optical_microscope" title="Optical microscope">light microscope</a>, other methods have to be employed to determine their structure. Common experimental methods include <a href="/wiki/X-ray_crystallography" title="X-ray crystallography">X-ray crystallography</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protein_NMR" title="Protein NMR">NMR spectroscopy</a>, both of which can produce structural information at <a href="/wiki/Atom" title="Atom">atomic</a> resolution. However, NMR experiments are able to provide information from which a subset of distances between pairs of atoms can be estimated, and the final possible conformations for a protein are determined by solving a <a href="/wiki/Distance_geometry" title="Distance geometry">distance geometry</a> problem. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dual_polarisation_interferometry" title="Dual polarisation interferometry">Dual polarisation interferometry</a> is a quantitative analytical method for measuring the overall <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protein_conformation" title="Protein conformation">protein conformation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Conformational_change" title="Conformational change">conformational changes</a> due to interactions or other stimulus. <a href="/wiki/Circular_dichroism" title="Circular dichroism">Circular dichroism</a> is another laboratory technique for determining internal β-sheet / α-helical composition of proteins. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cryoelectron_microscopy" title="Cryoelectron microscopy">Cryoelectron microscopy</a> is used to produce lower-resolution structural information about very large protein complexes, including assembled <a href="/wiki/Virus" title="Virus">viruses</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brandon_1999_29-3"><a href="#cite_note-Brandon_1999-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 340–41">: 340–41 </span></sup> a variant known as <a href="/wiki/Electron_crystallography" title="Electron crystallography">electron crystallography</a> can also produce high-resolution information in some cases, especially for two-dimensional crystals of membrane proteins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gonen2005_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gonen2005-69">[69]</a></sup> Solved structures are usually deposited in the <a href="/wiki/Protein_Data_Bank" title="Protein Data Bank">Protein Data Bank</a> (PDB), a freely available resource from which structural data about thousands of proteins can be obtained in the form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cartesian_coordinates" title="Cartesian coordinates">Cartesian coordinates</a> for each atom in the protein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Standley2008_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Standley2008-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many more gene sequences are known than protein structures. Further, the set of solved structures is biased toward proteins that can be easily subjected to the conditions required in <a href="/wiki/X-ray_crystallography" title="X-ray crystallography">X-ray crystallography</a>, one of the major structure determination methods. In particular, globular proteins are comparatively easy to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crystallize" title="Crystallize">crystallize</a> in preparation for X-ray crystallography. Membrane proteins and large protein complexes, by contrast, are difficult to crystallize and are underrepresented in the PDB.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walian2004_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walian2004-71">[71]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Structural_genomics" title="Structural genomics">Structural genomics</a> initiatives have attempted to remedy these deficiencies by systematically solving representative structures of major fold classes. <a href="/wiki/Protein_structure_prediction" title="Protein structure prediction">Protein structure prediction</a> methods attempt to provide a means of generating a plausible structure for proteins whose structures have not been experimentally determined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sleator2012_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sleator2012-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure_prediction">Structure prediction</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:225_Peptide_Bond-01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1886" data-file-width="2219" decoding="async" height="297" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/225_Peptide_Bond-01.jpg/350px-225_Peptide_Bond-01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/225_Peptide_Bond-01.jpg/525px-225_Peptide_Bond-01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/225_Peptide_Bond-01.jpg/700px-225_Peptide_Bond-01.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:225_Peptide_Bond-01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Constituent amino-acids can be analyzed to predict secondary, tertiary and quaternary protein structure, in this case hemoglobin containing <a href="/wiki/Heme" title="Heme">heme</a> units</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Protein_structure_prediction" title="Protein structure prediction">Protein structure prediction</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_protein_structure_prediction_software" title="List of protein structure prediction software">List of protein structure prediction software</a></div>
<p>Complementary to the field of structural genomics, <i>protein structure prediction</i> develops efficient <a href="/wiki/Mathematical_model" title="Mathematical model">mathematical models</a> of proteins to computationally predict the molecular formations in theory, instead of detecting structures with laboratory observation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhang2008_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zhang2008-73">[73]</a></sup> The most successful type of structure prediction, known as <a href="/wiki/Homology_modeling" title="Homology modeling">homology modeling</a>, relies on the existence of a "template" structure with sequence similarity to the protein being modeled; structural genomics' goal is to provide sufficient representation in solved structures to model most of those that remain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Xiang2006_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Xiang2006-74">[74]</a></sup> Although producing accurate models remains a challenge when only distantly related template structures are available, it has been suggested that <a href="/wiki/Sequence_alignment" title="Sequence alignment">sequence alignment</a> is the bottleneck in this process, as quite accurate models can be produced if a "perfect" sequence alignment is known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zhang2005_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zhang2005-75">[75]</a></sup> Many structure prediction methods have served to inform the emerging field of <a href="/wiki/Protein_engineering" title="Protein engineering">protein engineering</a>, in which novel protein folds have already been designed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kuhlman2003_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kuhlman2003-76">[76]</a></sup> Also proteins (in eukaryotes ~33%) contain large unstructured but biologically functional segments and can be classified as <a href="/wiki/Intrinsically_disordered_proteins" title="Intrinsically disordered proteins">intrinsically disordered proteins</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> Predicting and analysing protein disorder is, therefore, an important part of protein structure characterisation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TompaFersht2009_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-TompaFersht2009-78">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bioinformatics">Bioinformatics</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bioinformatics" title="Bioinformatics">Bioinformatics</a></div>
<p>A vast array of computational methods have been developed to analyze the structure, function and evolution of proteins. The development of such tools has been driven by the large amount of genomic and proteomic data available for a variety of organisms, including the <a href="/wiki/Human_genome" title="Human genome">human genome</a>. It is simply impossible to study all proteins experimentally, hence only a few are subjected to laboratory experiments while computational tools are used to extrapolate to similar proteins. Such <a href="/wiki/Sequence_homology" title="Sequence homology">homologous proteins</a> can be efficiently identified in distantly related organisms by <a href="/wiki/Sequence_alignment" title="Sequence alignment">sequence alignment</a>. Genome and gene sequences can be searched by a variety of tools for certain properties. <a href="/wiki/Sequence_profiling_tool" title="Sequence profiling tool">Sequence profiling tools</a> can find <a href="/wiki/Restriction_enzyme" title="Restriction enzyme">restriction enzyme</a> sites, <a href="/wiki/Open_reading_frame" title="Open reading frame">open reading frames</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nucleotide" title="Nucleotide">nucleotide</a> sequences, and predict <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secondary_structure" title="Secondary structure">secondary structures</a>. <a href="/wiki/Phylogenetic_tree" title="Phylogenetic tree">Phylogenetic trees</a> can be constructed and <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolutionary</a> hypotheses developed using special software like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ClustalW" title="ClustalW">ClustalW</a> regarding the ancestry of modern organisms and the genes they express. The field of <a href="/wiki/Bioinformatics" title="Bioinformatics">bioinformatics</a> is now indispensable for the analysis of genes and proteins.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_silico_simulation_of_dynamical_processes">In silico simulation of dynamical processes</span></h3>
<p>A more complex computational problem is the prediction of intermolecular interactions, such as in <a href="/wiki/Docking_(molecular)" title="Docking (molecular)">molecular docking</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ritchie2008_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ritchie2008-79">[79]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Protein_folding" title="Protein folding">protein folding</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protein%E2%80%93protein_interaction" title="Protein–protein interaction">protein–protein interaction</a> and chemical reactivity. Mathematical models to simulate these dynamical processes involve <a href="/wiki/Molecular_mechanics" title="Molecular mechanics">molecular mechanics</a>, in particular, <a href="/wiki/Molecular_dynamics" title="Molecular dynamics">molecular dynamics</a>. In this regard, <i><a href="/wiki/In_silico" title="In silico">in silico</a></i> simulations discovered the folding of small α-helical <a href="/wiki/Protein_domain" title="Protein domain">protein domains</a> such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Villin" title="Villin">villin</a> headpiece,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zagrovic2002_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zagrovic2002-80">[80]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a> accessory protein<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Herges2005_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Herges2005-81">[81]</a></sup> and hybrid methods combining standard molecular dynamics with <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">quantum mechanical</a> mathematics have explored the electronic states of <a href="/wiki/Rhodopsin" title="Rhodopsin">rhodopsins</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoffman2006_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoffman2006-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>Beyond classical molecular dynamics, <a href="/wiki/Quantum_dynamics" title="Quantum dynamics">quantum dynamics</a> methods allow the simulation of proteins in atomistic detail with an accurate description of quantum mechanical effects. Examples include the multi-layer <a href="/wiki/Multi-configuration_time-dependent_Hartree" title="Multi-configuration time-dependent Hartree">multi-configuration time-dependent Hartree </a> (MCTDH) method and the <a href="/wiki/Hierarchical_equations_of_motion" title="Hierarchical equations of motion">hierarchical equations of motion</a> (HEOM) approach, which have been applied to plant cryptochromes<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gatti2018_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gatti2018-83">[83]</a></sup> and bacteria light-harvesting complexes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schulten2012_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schulten2012-84">[84]</a></sup> respectively. Both quantum and classical mechanical simulations of biological-scale systems are extremely computationally demanding, so <a href="/wiki/Distributed_computing" title="Distributed computing">distributed computing</a> initiatives (for example, the <a href="/wiki/Folding@home" title="Folding@home">Folding@home</a> project<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scheraga2007_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-Scheraga2007-85">[85]</a></sup>) facilitate the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_modeling_on_GPU" title="Molecular modeling on GPU">molecular modeling</a> by exploiting advances in <a href="/wiki/Graphics_processing_unit" title="Graphics processing unit">GPU</a> parallel processing and <a href="/wiki/Monte_Carlo_method" title="Monte Carlo method">Monte Carlo</a> techniques.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_analysis">Chemical analysis</span></h3>
<p>The total nitrogen content of organic matter is mainly formed by the amino groups in proteins. The Total Kjeldahl Nitrogen (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TKN" title="TKN">TKN</a>) is a measure of nitrogen widely used in the analysis of (waste) water, soil, food, feed and organic matter in general. As the name suggests, the <a href="/wiki/Kjeldahl_method" title="Kjeldahl method">Kjeldahl method</a> is applied. More sensitive methods are available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutrition">Nutrition</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Protein_(nutrient)" title="Protein (nutrient)">Protein (nutrient)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Protein_quality" title="Protein quality">Protein quality</a></div>
<p>Most <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a> and plants can biosynthesize all 20 standard <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amino_acids" title="Amino acids">amino acids</a>, while animals (including humans) must obtain some of the amino acids from the <a href="/wiki/Diet_(nutrition)" title="Diet (nutrition)">diet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Voet_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Voet-42">[42]</a></sup> The amino acids that an organism cannot synthesize on its own are referred to as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Essential_amino_acids" title="Essential amino acids">essential amino acids</a>. Key enzymes that synthesize certain amino acids are not present in animals—such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aspartokinase" title="Aspartokinase">aspartokinase</a>, which catalyses the first step in the synthesis of <a href="/wiki/Lysine" title="Lysine">lysine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methionine" title="Methionine">methionine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Threonine" title="Threonine">threonine</a> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aspartate" title="Aspartate">aspartate</a>. If amino acids are present in the environment, microorganisms can conserve energy by taking up the amino acids from their surroundings and <a href="/wiki/Downregulation_and_upregulation" title="Downregulation and upregulation">downregulating</a> their biosynthetic pathways.
</p><p>In animals, amino acids are obtained through the consumption of foods containing protein. Ingested proteins are then broken down into amino acids through <a href="/wiki/Digestion" title="Digestion">digestion</a>, which typically involves <a href="/wiki/Denaturation_(biochemistry)" title="Denaturation (biochemistry)">denaturation</a> of the protein through exposure to <a href="/wiki/Acid" title="Acid">acid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrolysis" title="Hydrolysis">hydrolysis</a> by enzymes called <a href="/wiki/Protease" title="Protease">proteases</a>. Some ingested amino acids are used for protein biosynthesis, while others are converted to <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> through <a href="/wiki/Gluconeogenesis" title="Gluconeogenesis">gluconeogenesis</a>, or fed into the <a href="/wiki/Citric_acid_cycle" title="Citric acid cycle">citric acid cycle</a>. This use of protein as a fuel is particularly important under <a href="/wiki/Starvation" title="Starvation">starvation</a> conditions as it allows the body's own proteins to be used to support life, particularly those found in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muscle" title="Muscle">muscle</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BrosnanJ_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-BrosnanJ-88">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>In animals such as dogs and cats, protein maintains the health and quality of the skin by promoting hair follicle growth and keratinization, and thus reducing the likelihood of skin problems producing malodours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson_1998_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watson_1998-89">[89]</a></sup> Poor-quality proteins also have a role regarding gastrointestinal health, increasing the potential for flatulence and odorous compounds in dogs because when proteins reach the colon in an undigested state, they are fermented producing hydrogen sulfide gas, indole, and skatole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Case_2010_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Case_2010-90">[90]</a></sup> Dogs and cats digest animal proteins better than those from plants, but products of low-quality animal origin are poorly digested, including skin, feathers, and connective tissue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Case_2010_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-Case_2010-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Databases_and_projects">Databases and projects</span></h3>
<ul><li><a class="external text" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sites/entrez?db=protein" rel="nofollow">NCBI Entrez Protein database</a></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sites/entrez?db=structure" rel="nofollow">NCBI Protein Structure database</a></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20060424071622/http://www.hprd.org/" rel="nofollow">Human Protein Reference Database</a></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20070314135408/http://www.humanproteinpedia.org/" rel="nofollow">Human Proteinpedia</a></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://folding.stanford.edu/" rel="nofollow">Folding@Home (Stanford University)</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20120908075542/http://folding.stanford.edu/English/HomePage" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 2012-09-08 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a></li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.pdbe.org/" rel="nofollow">Protein Databank in Europe</a> (see also <a class="external text" href="http://www.pdbe.org/quips" rel="nofollow">PDBeQuips</a>, short articles and tutorials on interesting PDB structures)</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.rcsb.org/" rel="nofollow">Research Collaboratory for Structural Bioinformatics</a> (see also <a class="external text" href="http://www.rcsb.org/pdb/static.do?p=education_discussion/molecule_of_the_month/index.html" rel="nofollow">Molecule of the Month</a> <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20200724151351/https://www.rcsb.org/pdb/static.do?p=education_discussion%2Fmolecule_of_the_month%2Findex.html" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> 2020-07-24 at the <a href="/wiki/Wayback_Machine" title="Wayback Machine">Wayback Machine</a>, presenting short accounts on selected proteins from the PDB)</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="http://www.proteopedia.org/" rel="nofollow">Proteopedia – Life in 3D</a>: rotatable, zoomable 3D model with wiki annotations for every known protein molecular structure.</li>
<li><a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20080608183902/http://www.expasy.uniprot.org/" rel="nofollow">UniProt the Universal Protein Resource</a></li></ul>

<li><a href="/wiki/Central_dogma_of_molecular_biology" title="Central dogma of molecular biology">Central dogma</a>
